<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2014 46:7"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-06-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Scott Jackson, Jeremy Schmutz, Phillip McClean and colleagues report the genome sequence of the common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris) and resequenced wild individuals and landraces from Mesoamerican and Andean gene pools, showing that common bean underwent two independent domestications. Common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) is the most important grain legume for human consumption and has a role in sustainable agriculture owing to its ability to fix atmospheric nitrogen. We assembled 473 Mb of the 587-Mb genome and genetically anchored 98% of this sequence in 11 chromosome-scale pseudomolecules. We compared the genome for the common bean against the soybean genome to find changes in soybean resulting from polyploidy. Using resequencing of 60 wild individuals and 100 landraces from the genetically differentiated Mesoamerican and Andean gene pools, we confirmed 2 independent domestications from genetic pools that diverged before human colonization. Less than 10% of the 74 Mb of sequence putatively involved in domestication was shared by the two domestication events. We identified a set of genes linked with increased leaf and seed size and combined these results with quantitative trait locus data from Mesoamerican cultivars. Genes affected by domestication may be useful for genomics-enabled crop improvement."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i> L.) is the most important grain legume for human consumption and has a role in sustainable agriculture owing to its ability to fix atmospheric nitrogen. We assembled 473 Mb of the 587-Mb genome and genetically anchored 98% of this sequence in 11 chromosome-scale pseudomolecules. We compared the genome for the common bean against the soybean genome to find changes in soybean resulting from polyploidy. Using resequencing of 60 wild individuals and 100 landraces from the genetically differentiated Mesoamerican and Andean gene pools, we confirmed 2 independent domestications from genetic pools that diverged before human colonization. Less than 10% of the 74 Mb of sequence putatively involved in domestication was shared by the two domestication events. We identified a set of genes linked with increased leaf and seed size and combined these results with quantitative trait locus data from Mesoamerican cultivars. Genes affected by domestication may be useful for genomics-enabled crop improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Common bean (<i>P. vulgaris</i> L.) is a crop of major societal importance and is a major source of protein and essential nutrients. Worldwide, common bean is the most consumed legume, providing up to 15% of total daily calories and 36% of total daily protein in parts of Africa and the Americas (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Sec22">URLs</a>). More than 200 million people in sub-Saharan Africa depend on the common bean as a primary staple. It has many health-beneficial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Anderson, J.W. et al. Hypocholesterolemic effects of oat-bran or bean intake for hypercholesterolemic men. Am. J. Clin. Nutr. 40, 1146â1155 (1984)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1063">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Geil, P. &amp; Anderson, J. Nutrition and health implications of dry beans: a review. J. Am. Coll. Nutr. 13, 549â558 (1994)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1066">2</a></sup> nutrients whose concentrations are heritable<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cichy, K.A., Caldas, G.V., Snapp, S.S. &amp; Blair, M.W. QTL analysis of seed iron, zinc, and phosphorus levels in an Andean bean population. Crop Sci. 49, 1742â1750 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1070">3</a></sup>, and increasing the concentrations of these nutrients is a breeding objective worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Beebe, S. Common bean breeding in the tropics. Plant Breed. Rev. 36, 357â426 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1074">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Multiple lines of evidence have shown that wild common bean is organized in two geographically isolated and genetically differentiated wild gene pools (Mesoamerican and Andean) that diverged from a common ancestral wild population more than 100,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mamidi, S. et al. Demographic factors shaped diversity in the two gene pools of wild common bean Phaseolus vulgaris L. Heredity 110, 267â276 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1081">5</a></sup>. From these wild gene pools, nearly 8,000 years ago, common bean was independently domesticated in what is now Mexico and in South America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bitocchi, E. et al. Molecular analysis of the parallel domestication of the common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris) in Mesoamerica and the Andes. New Phytol. 197, 300â313 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1085">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bitocchi, E. et al. Mesoamerican origin of the common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) is revealed by sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 109, E788âE796 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1088">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gepts, P., Osborn, T., Rashka, K. &amp; Bliss, F. Phaseolin-protein variability in wild forms and landraces of the common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris): evidence for multiple centers of domestication. Econ. Bot. 40, 451â468 (1986)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1091">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mamidi, S. et al. Investigation of the domestication of common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris) using multilocus sequence data. Funct. Plant Biol. 38, 953â967 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1094">9</a></sup>, and these domestication events were followed by local adaptations resulting in landraces with distinct characteristics. In what is now Mexico, common bean was likely domesticated concurrently with maize as part of the 'milpa' cropping system (featuring common bean along with maize and squash), which was adopted throughout the Americas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zizumbo-Villarreal, D. &amp; Colunga-GarcÃ­aMarÃ­n, P. Origin of agriculture and plant domestication in West Mesoamerica. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 57, 813â825 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1098">10</a></sup>. Domestication led to morphological changes, including increased seed and leaf sizes, changes in growth habit and photoperiod responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Singh, S.P., Gepts, P. &amp; Debouck, D.G. Races of common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris, Fabaceae). Econ. Bot. 45, 379â396 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1102">11</a></sup>, and variation in seed coat color and pattern that distinguish culturally adapted classes of beans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="McClean, P.E., Lee, R., Otto, C., Gepts, P. &amp; Bassett, M. Molecular and phenotypic mapping of genes controlling seed coat pattern and color in common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.). J. Hered. 93, 148â152 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1106">12</a></sup>.</p><p>Independent domestication events, starting from distinct gene pools of a single species, provide experimental replication not typically found in domestication or evolutionary studies. It is possible to deduce domestication history on a genome-wide scale and examine the roles of parallel evolution and introgression during the domestication of two independent lineages within a single species. Here, to understand the history of these complicated domestication events and their implications for modern bean crop improvement, we report a genome sequence for an Andean ecotype of common bean and an analysis of genetic variation in accessions ranging from Mexico to the southern range of the species in Argentina. In addition, comparative genomics with soybean (<i>Glycine max</i>), a closely related crop, identified effects of shared and lineage-dependent polyploidies on gene fractionation and recent transposable element expansion in the common bean.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Reference genome and analysis</h3><p>To obtain a high-quality reference genome, we sequenced an inbred landrace line of <i>P. vulgaris</i> (G19833) derived from the Andean pool (Race Peru) using a whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy that combined multiple linear libraries (18.6Ã assembled sequence coverage) and ten paired libraries of varying insert sizes (1.8Ã assembled) sequenced with the Roche 454 platform together with 24.1 Gb of Illumina-sequenced fragment libraries. For longer-range linkage, we also end sequenced three fosmid libraries and two BAC libraries on the Sanger platform (0.54Ã long-insert pairs) for a total assembled sequence coverage level of 21.0Ã (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Tables 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">2</a>). The resulting assembled sequences were organized into 11 chromosomal pseudomolecules by integration with a dense GoldenGate- and Infinium-based SNP map of 7,015 markers typed on 267 F<sub>2</sub> lines from a Stampede Ã Red Hawk cross and a similar set of Infinium markers and 261 SSRs (simple sequence repeats) typed on 88 F<sub>5</sub>-derived recombinant inbred lines (RILs) derived from the same cross (P.B.C. and Q.S., unpublished data). Additional refinements to the pseudomolecules were made on the basis of synteny with soybean (<i>G. max</i>), where allowed by available map data. Almost all of these changes were made in pericentromeric regions, where recombination is generally too limited to resolve the ordering and orientation of small scaffolds. The pseudomolecules included 468.2 Mb of mapped sequence in 240 scaffolds. The total release includes 472.5 Mb of the <span class="stix">â¼</span>587-Mb genome (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Sec22">URLs</a>), with half of the assembled nucleotides in contigs longer than 39.5 kb (contig N50) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 3</a>). To annotate the chromosomal assembly, we combined Sanger-derived EST resources and a substantial amount of new RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) reads (727 million reads from 11 tissues and developmental stages; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 4</a>) with homology-based and <i>de novo</i> gene prediction approaches. The resulting annotation includes 27,197 protein-coding loci, including 4,491 alternative transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 5</a>), an underestimate that will increase with additional transcriptomes and analyses. Most of these genes (91%) were retained in synteny blocks with <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>We identified recent transposable element activity and expansions of transposon numbers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Figs. 1â3</a>). Although recently diverged repeats could not be annotated directly from Roche 454 pyrosequencing data, extensive BAC-end and fosmid-end sequence data and a dense genetic map allowed us to position 99.6% of genic sequences and to link into those genes embedded in regions dense with transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Figs. 4â14</a>). Centromere and pericentromeric regions were primarily repetitive, and, similar to in other sequenced genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1176">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1179">14</a></sup>, these pericentromeric genomic regions were recombinationally inert (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Using a threshold of 2 Mb/cM to identify transitions into pericentromeric regions, pericentromeres spanned <span class="stix">â¼</span>54% of the genome and had an average recombination rate of 4,350 kb/cM versus 220 kb/cM in the euchromatic arms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 7</a>). The pericentromeres were primarily repetitive but, owing to their size, still contained 26.5% of the genes.</p><p>The majority of the repetitive elements in the genome were long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, and we identified 2,668 complete LTR retrotransposons and classified them into 165 families, including 65 <i>Ty</i>1-<i>copia</i>, 78 <i>Ty</i>3-<i>gypsy</i> and 22 unclassified families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Tables 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">9</a>). Although there were ancient elements that inserted into the genome more than 10 million years ago, <span class="stix">â¼</span>75% (2,011/2,668) of the LTR retroelements integrated into <i>P. vulgaris</i> within the last 2 million years (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Notably, the insertion times of 20% (543/2,668) of the elements were more recent than 0.5 million years agoâthis is likely an underestimate, as our sequencing approach is biased against the annotation of completely identical LTRs. These results were similar to those in soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Meyers, B.C., Kaushik, S. &amp; Nandety, R.S. Evolving disease resistance genes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 129â134 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1221">15</a></sup> and suggest that LTR retrotransposons underwent recent amplification events in both legumes. The 165 LTR retrotransposon families varied in the copy number of complete elements: more than 78% (130/165) of the families had fewer than 10 complete retroelements, whereas 11 families had more than 50 complete elements and contained 63% (1,690/2,668) of the complete elements in the <i>P. vulgaris</i> genome. Some families showed extremely high copy numbers; for example, the pvRetroS2 family contained 446 complete elements (likely an underestimate, as some elements would not have been annotated uniquely).</p><p>We observed dense clusters of resistance-associated genes in the common bean genome. The majority of putative resistance-associated genes in plants encode nucleotide-binding and leucine-rich repeat domains and are collectively known as NB-LRR (NL) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Meyers, B.C., Kaushik, S. &amp; Nandety, R.S. Evolving disease resistance genes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 129â134 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1231">15</a></sup>. We identified 376 NL genes, of which 106 encoded an N-terminal Toll/interleukin-1 receptor (TIR)-like domain (TNLs) and 108 encoded an N-terminal coiled-coil domain (CNLs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 10</a>). The majority of NL sequences were physically organized in complex clusters, often located at the ends of chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). In particular, three large clusters were located at the ends of chromosomes Pv04, Pv10 and Pv11 and contained more than 40 NL genes that were enriched for CNL (Pv04 and Pv11) or TNL (Pv10) genes that colocalized with previously mapped genes related to disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Geffroy, V. et al. Molecular analysis of a large subtelomeric nucleotide-binding-siteâleucine-rich-repeat family in two representative genotypes of the major gene pools of Phaseolus vulgaris. Genetics 181, 405â419 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1241">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Geffroy, V. et al. Identification of an ancestral resistance gene cluster involved in the coevolution process between Phaseolus vulgaris and its fungal pathogen Colletotrichum lindemuthianum. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 12, 774â784 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1244">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Innes, R.W. et al. Differential accumulation of retroelements and diversification of NB-LRR disease resistance genes in duplicated regions following polyploidy in the ancestor of soybean. Plant Physiol. 148, 1740â1759 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1247">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Chen, N.W.G. et al. Specific resistances against Pseudomonas syringae effectors AvrB and AvrRpm1 have evolved differently in common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris), soybean (Glycine max), and Arabidopsis thaliana. New Phytol. 187, 941â956 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1250">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Geffroy, V. et al. A family of LRR sequences in the vicinity of the Co-2 locus for anthracnose resistance in Phaseolus vulgaris and its potential use in marker-assisted selection. Theor. Appl. Genet. 96, 494â502 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1253">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Miklas, P.N., Kelly, J.D., Beebe, S.E. &amp; Blair, M.W. Common bean breeding for resistance against biotic and abiotic stresses: from classical to MAS breeding. Euphytica 147, 105â131 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1256">21</a></sup>. Local tandem duplications and ectopic recombination between clusters are involved in the evolution of these NL gene clusters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="David, P. et al. A nomadic subtelomeric disease resistance gene cluster in common bean. Plant Physiol. 151, 1048â1065 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1260">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparison of genome changes in sister legume species</h3><p><i>P. vulgaris</i> (common bean) and <i>G. max</i> (soybean) diverged <span class="stix">â¼</span>19.2 million years ago but shared a whole-genome duplication (WGD) event <span class="stix">â¼</span>56.5 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P.S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M.F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the Tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1277">23</a></sup>. <i>G. max</i> experienced an independent WGD <span class="stix">â¼</span>10 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1284">14</a></sup>. These events were evident in plots of synonymous changes in coding sequences (<i>K</i>s) between and within these genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>), which also showed that <i>P. vulgaris</i> has evolved more rapidly than <i>G. max</i> since they split from their last common ancestor. Assuming a divergence time of <span class="stix">â¼</span>19.2 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P.S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M.F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the Tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1301">23</a></sup>, the <i>K</i>s value (synonymous substitution rate) for <i>P. vulgaris</i> was 1.4 times that of <i>G. max</i> (8.46 Ã 10<sup>â9</sup> versus 5.85 Ã 10<sup>â9</sup> substitutions/year).</p><p>We identified orthologous <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>G. max</i> genes using synteny and <i>K</i>s values as criteria (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Consistent with earlier work, there was extensive synteny between <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>G. max</i>, except in pericentromeric regions, where microcollinearity was often stretched out and thinned owing to genomic expansion in one or both genomes. Typically, two chromosomal blocks in <i>G. max</i> mapped to a single region of <i>P. vulgaris</i> owing to the most recent WGD in <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1353">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gill, N. et al. Molecular and chromosomal evidence for allopolyploidy in soybean. Plant Physiol. 151, 1167â1174 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1356">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="McClean, P.E., Mamidi, S., McConnell, M., Chikara, S. &amp; Lee, R. Synteny mapping between common bean and soybean reveals extensive blocks of shared loci. BMC Genomics 11, 184 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1359">25</a></sup>. Most of the <i>P. vulgaris</i> genes (91%; 24,861) were in identifiable synteny blocks in <i>G. max</i>, and 57% were in synteny blocks in <i>P. vulgaris</i> itselfâa result of the ancient WGD event 55 million years ago. Within synteny blocks, the <i>G. max</i>â<i>G. max</i> duplication had a mean of 33 genes/block, whereas the older, shared <i>P. vulgaris</i>â<i>G. max</i> WGD event had an average of 14 genes/block.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Structure of the P. vulgaris genome and synteny with the G. max genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Structure of the <i>P. vulgaris</i> genome and synteny with the <i>G. max</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="760"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Gray lines connect duplicated genes. (<b>b</b>) Chromosome structure with centromeric and pericentromeric regions in black and gray, respectively (scale is in Mb). (<b>c</b>) Gene density in sliding windows of 1 Mb at 200-kb intervals. (<b>d</b>) Repeat density in sliding windows of 1 Mb at 200-kb intervals. (<b>e</b>) Recombination rate based on the genetic and physical mapping of 6,945 SNPs and SSRs. (<b>f</b>,<b>g</b>) First syntenic region (<b>f</b>) and second <i>G. max</i> syntenic region (<b>g</b>) due to a lineage-specific duplication resulting in two chromosome segments for every segment in <i>P. vulgaris</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of gene pools in common bean</h3><p>Mesoamerica has been suggested to be the center from which common bean originated, ultimately forming the distinct modern wild Andean and Mesoamerican gene pools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bitocchi, E. et al. Mesoamerican origin of the common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) is revealed by sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 109, E788âE796 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1455">7</a></sup>. To investigate the differentiation of these wild populations, we performed pooled resequencing of 30 individuals each from Mesoamerican and Andean wild populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Using <i>Ï</i> (the average pairwise nucleotide differences in a sample) and <i>Î¸</i> (the proportion of nucleotide polymorphisms in a sample), the Mesoamerican wild population (<i>Ï</i> (per bp) = 0.0061; <i>Î¸</i> (per bp) = 0.0041) was more diverse than the Andean wild population (<i>Ï</i> (per bp) = 0.0014; <i>Î¸</i> (per bp) = 0.0013). We used <span class="stix">â¼</span>663,000 polymorphic sites (at least 5 kb from a gene and not in a repeat sequence) to estimate demographic parameters using the joint allele frequency spectrum (Î´aÎ´i)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Gutenkunst, R.N., Hernandez, R.D., Williamson, S.H. &amp; Bustamante, C.D. Inferring the joint demographic history of multiple populations from multidimensional SNP frequency data. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000695 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1484">26</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Note</a>). The strong fixation index <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> of <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.34 between these two wild populations indicates that they have substantial allelic differentiation from each other. We estimated that divergence of the two wild pools occurred <span class="stix">â¼</span>165,000 years ago, with an ancestral effective population size of 168,000. This date is earlier than a previous estimate of <span class="stix">â¼</span>110,000 years ago but falls within the 95% confidence interval of the previous estimate, which was based on 13 loci from 24 wild genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mamidi, S. et al. Demographic factors shaped diversity in the two gene pools of wild common bean Phaseolus vulgaris L. Heredity 110, 267â276 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1496">5</a></sup>, but it is later than other estimates of <span class="stix">â¼</span>500,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="ChacÃ³n S, M.I., Pickersgill, B. &amp; Debouck, D.G. Domestication patterns in common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) and the origin of the Mesoamerican and Andean cultivated races. Theor. Appl. Genet. 110, 432â444 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1500">27</a></sup>. The whole-genome analysis resulted in a much tighter confidence interval of 146,000â184,000 years ago.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="737"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Geographic distribution of sampled genotypes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Demographic inference for the wild Andean gene pool suggested that it was derived from the wild Mesoamerican population with a founding population of only a few thousand individuals (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Note</a>). The wild Andean population showed no appreciable growth in effective population size for <span class="stix">â¼</span>76,000 years after founding, although there was continual asymmetric gene flow between the two wild populations, with a higher Mesoamerican-to-Andean migration rate (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 13</a>). The Andean population then underwent an exponential growth phase that began <span class="stix">â¼</span>90,000 years ago and has continued to the present. The strong predomestication bottleneck in the Andean population has been observed in previous analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bitocchi, E. et al. Mesoamerican origin of the common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) is revealed by sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 109, E788âE796 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1533">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kwak, M. &amp; Gepts, P. Structure of genetic diversity in the two major gene pools of common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L., Fabaceae). Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 979â992 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1536">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Rossi, M. et al. Linkage disequilibrium and population structure in wild and domesticated populations of Phaseolus vulgaris L. Evol. Appl. 2, 504â522 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1539">29</a></sup>; in contrast, however, no detectable bottleneck was found for the wild Mesoamerican gene pool.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolution and domestication of common bean."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Evolution and domestication of common bean.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="372"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Divergence of the wild Mesoamerican and Andean common bean pools. The wild Andean gene pool diverged from the wild Mesoamerican gene pool <span class="stix">â¼</span>165,000 years ago, with a small founding population and a strong bottleneck that lasted <span class="stix">â¼</span>76,000 years. The bottleneck was followed by an exponential growth phase extending to the present day. Asymmetric gene flow between the two pools had a key role in maintaining genetic diversity, especially in the Andean population, with average migration rates <i>M</i><sub>21</sub> = 0.135 (wild Mesoamerican to wild Andean) and <i>M</i><sub>12</sub> = 0.087 (wild Andean to wild Mesoamerican). This scenario conforms to the Mesoamerican origin model of the common bean, with an Andean bottleneck that predated domestication. (<i>n</i><sub>anc</sub>, size of ancestral population; <i>t</i><sub>div</sub>, start of bottleneck; <i>n</i><sub>b</sub>, size of bottleneck population; <i>t</i><sub>b</sub>, length of bottleneck) (<b>b</b>) Population genomic analysis based on SNP data from the resequencing of DNA pools for common bean. The size of the circle for each pool is proportional to the <i>Ï</i> value for the pool. For a reference, <i>Ï</i> = 0.0061 for the wild Mesoamerican (MA) pool. <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> statistics, representing the differentiation of any two pools, are noted on the lines (not proportional) connecting pools. Data are average statistics across all 10-kb/2-kb sliding/discarding windows with &lt;50% called bases. Land, landrace; N, north; S, south; C, central. (<b>c</b>) Variation in seed size in common bean. The seeds of wild Mesoamerican and Andean beans (two each) are smaller than the seeds corresponding to the reference genotype (G19833) and the multiple market classes of common beans grown in the United States (navy to light red kidney).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Domestication of common bean</h3><p>To characterize diversity and differentiation within and between the Mesoamerican and Andean landraces (early domesticates), we sequenced 4 pooled populations representing distinct Mesoamerican landraces and 2 pooled populations representing distinct Andean landraces (<i>n</i> = 7â26 landraces). These landraces represent subpopulations from Mexico, Central America and South America with low levels of admixture (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). Because the four Mesoamerican and two Andean landrace populations are representative of the diversity of the original domestication populations, we combined SNP data from these populations to create a composite Mesoamerican and a composite Andean landrace SNP data set, respectively, for further analysis. This approach allowed us to distinguish selection from random fixation across the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Rubin, C.-J. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals loci under selection during chicken domestication. Nature 464, 587â591 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1622">30</a></sup> and to search for signals associated with domestication events. The number of SNPs ranged from 8,890,318 for the wild Mesoamerican subpopulation to 1,397,405 SNPs for the Andean landrace subpopulation from Peru (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 14</a>), and <span class="stix">â¼</span>16% of these SNPs were within genes.</p><p>To characterize variation among the populations, we calculated diversity (<i>Ï</i>) and population differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) statistics using data averaged over 10-kb windows with a 2-kb slide (10-kb/2-kb windows; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Whereas the Mesoamerican landraces were less diverse than the wild Mesoamerican population, Andean landrace populations were more diverse than the wild Andean population, possibly owing to admixture with Mesoamerican populations and/or <i>de novo</i> mutation within the Andean gene pool. Diversity was further reduced within the Mesoamerican Central American and southern Andean landraces, suggesting that these subpopulations underwent additional selection that might correspond to local adaptation.</p><p>Multiple results point to independent domestication events in the Mesoamerican and Andean gene pools, a feature observed for only a few modern crops. We characterized domestication of common bean at the genomic level by comparing wild and landrace populations across 10-kb/2-kb sliding windows, selecting windows that met strict composite criteria that required they be in the top 90% of the population's empirical distribution for both <i>Ï</i><sub>wild</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>landrace</sub> ratios and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig3">Figs. 3b,c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig4">4</a>). We observed 930 windows in Mesoamerican populations (totaling 74 Mb of sequence) with both low diversity and high differentiation. Because low diversity and high differentiation are two features of selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Doebley, J.F., Gaut, B.S. &amp; Smith, B.D. The molecular genetics of crop domestication. Cell 127, 1309â1321 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1667">31</a></sup>, we consider these to be selection windows. Of these windows, 209 that were longer than 100 kb accounted for 70.1% of the total selection distance. Among the 750 selection windows in Andean populations exhibiting low diversity and high differentiation, 172 that were longer than 100 kb covered 69.8% of the total selection distance (60 Mb). As expected for independent Mesoamerican and Andean domestication events, these selection regions were distinct. Within the Mesoamerican landrace population, chromosomes Pv02, Pv07 and Pv09 accounted for 43% of the length (32.338 Mb), with 33.3% of chromosome Pv09 showing signatures of selection, whereas the Andean domestication event primarily involved chromosomes Pv01, Pv02 and Pv10 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Interestingly, only 7.234 Mb of the regions predicted to be involved in domestication were shared by the two gene pools, suggesting different genetic routes to domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Differentiation and reduction in diversity during the domestication of common bean."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Differentiation and reduction in diversity during the domestication of common bean.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="507"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>,<b>b</b>) Genome-wide view in 10-kb/2-kb sliding windows of differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) and reduction in diversity (<i>Ï</i> ratio) statistics associated with domestication within the common bean Mesoamerican (<b>a</b>) and Andean (<b>b</b>) gene pools. Log<sub>10</sub> <i>Ï</i> ratios less than zero are not shown. Lines represent the 90%, 95% and 99% tails for the empirical distribution of each statistic.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified candidate genes associated with domestication using the same criteria applied to find selection windows (requiring that they be in the top 90% of the pool's empirical distribution for both <i>Ï</i><sub>wild</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>landrace</sub> ratios and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values). We identified 1,835 Mesoamerican and 748 Andean candidate genes associated with domestication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM58">Supplementary Tables 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM59">17</a>), and all candidates had a negative Tajima's <i>D</i> value, indicating positive selection. Most notably, only 59 of the candidate genes (3% of the Mesoamerican and 8% of the Andean candidates) were shared by the 2 landrace populations. For the 59 common candidates, the mean <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> value was 0.67, suggesting selection on different alleles or the appearance of unique mutations in the two gene pools. This finding is consistent with evidence at the <i>PvTFL1y</i> determinancy locus that was independently derived in each gene pool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Repinski, S.L., Kwak, M. &amp; Gepts, P. The common bean growth habit gene PvTFL1y is a functional homolog of Arabidopsis TFL1. Theor. App. Genet. 124, 1539â1547 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1751">32</a></sup> but contrasts with evidence in rice, where a domestication locus appeared uniquely in one gene pool, <i>indica</i> or <i>japonica</i>, and was transferred to the other pools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Sweeney, M.T. et al. Global dissemination of a single mutation conferring white pericarp in rice. PLoS Genet. 3, e133 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1762">33</a></sup>. Most Mesoamerican candidate genes (<i>n</i> = 1,561; 85%) were located in 10-kb selection windows, whereas only 48.1% of the Andean candidate genes were within such windows (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 18</a>). The effects of domestication were uneven across the Mesoamerican subpopulations: we detected only 418 candidates in the Mesoamerican Central American landrace population compared to 1,424 candidates in the Mesoamerican Mexican landraces. The fact that only 33 of these genes were shared by these 2 subpopulations indicates unique evolutionary trajectories among subpopulations of the Mesoamerican gene pool. Within the Andean gene pool, none of the candidate genes from the northern and southern Andean landrace populations were shared. These results demonstrate that the sexually compatible Mesoamerican and Andean lineages with similar morphologies and life cycles underwent independent selection upon distinct sets of genes. This is in contrast to the situation in rice, where many major domestication genes were shared by gene flow between the <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i> types<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Huang, X. et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature 490, 497â501 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1779">34</a></sup>.</p><p>Domestication had distinct effects on genes involved in flowering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Fornara, F., de Montaigu, A. &amp; Coupland, G. SnapShot: control of flowering in Arabidopsis thaliana. Cell 141, 550 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1787">35</a></sup> in the two gene pools. Whereas the principal floral integrator genes <i>SOC1</i> and <i>FT</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Fornara, F., de Montaigu, A. &amp; Coupland, G. SnapShot: control of flowering in Arabidopsis thaliana. Cell 141, 550 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1796">35</a></sup> were not candidate domestication genes in either pool, 25 Mesoamerican and 13 Andean genes that are in pathways that control these 2 genes were candidate genes for domestication. For example, within the vernalization pathway, orthologs of <i>VRN1</i> (<i>Phvul.003G033400</i>) and <i>VRN2</i> (<i>Phvul.002G000500</i>) were Mesoamerican candidate genes, and orthologs of <i>FRL1</i> (<i>Phvul.006G053200</i>) and <i>TFL2</i> (<i>Phvul.009G117500</i>) were Andean candidate genes. <i>COP1</i> encodes a photoperiod pathway regulator that controls <i>FT</i> through <i>CO</i>. The Mesoamerican ortholog of <i>COP1</i> was a candidate domestication gene, and <i>Phvul.006G165300</i>, a <i>CUL4</i> ortholog that encodes a protein that is part of a complex that along with COP1 regulates <i>CO</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chen, H. et al. Arabidopsis CULLIN4-damaged DNA binding protein 1 interacts with CONSTITUTIVELY PHOTOMORPHOGENIC1âSUPPRESSOR OF PHYA complexes to regulate photomorphogenesis and flowering time. Plant Cell 22, 108â123 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1847">36</a></sup>, was an Andean candidate gene for domestication. This finding demonstrates independent selection on genes encoding different members of the same protein complex. The only shared domestication candidates were <i>Phvul.007065600</i>, an ortholog of <i>AGL42</i>, which regulates flowering through the gibberellin pathway, and <i>Phvul.009G203400</i>, an ortholog of <i>FUL</i>, which regulates <i>SOC1</i>.</p><p>Increased plant size is typically associated with plant domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Gepts, P. Crop domestication as a long-term selection experiment. Plant Breed. Rev. 24, 1â44 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1870">37</a></sup>, and multiple Mesoamerican candidate genes influence this trait. <i>Phvul.011G213300</i> is an ortholog of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana BB</i>, a component of the ubiquitin ligase degradation pathway that controls flower and stem size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Disch, S. et al. The E3 ubiquitin ligase BIG BROTHER controls Arabidopsis organ size in a dosage-dependent manner. Curr. Biol. 16, 272â279 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1880">38</a></sup>, and <i>Phvul.009G040200</i> is an ortholog of <i>BIN4</i>, which regulates cell expansion and final plant size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Breuer, C. et al. BIN4, a novel component of the plant DNA topoisomerase VI complex, is required for endoreduplication in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 19, 3655â3668 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1891">39</a></sup>. Multiple candidate genes for domestication were also components of nitrogen metabolism pathways, which directly affect plant size. The Mesoamerican candidate gene <i>Phvul.008G168000</i> encodes nitrate reductase, a critical element for plant and seed growth, which genetically maps to the <i>SW8.2</i> quantitative trait locus (QTL) for seed weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="PÃ©rez-Vega, E. et al. Mapping of QTLs for morpho-agronomic and seed quality traits in a RIL population of common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 120, 1367â1380 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1901">40</a></sup>. Other candidate genes for domestication involved in nitrogen metabolism included the Mesoamerican (<i>Phvul.005G132200</i>) and Andean (<i>Phvul.002G242900</i>) nitrogen transporters and the Mesoamerican asparagine synthase (<i>Phvul.006G069300</i>).</p><p>Increased seed size is a major phenotypic shift associated with the domestication of the common bean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Koinange, E.M., Singh, S.P. &amp; Gepts, P. Genetic control of the domestication syndrome in common bean. Crop Sci. 36, 1037â1045 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1918">41</a></sup> and other legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Weeden, N.F. Genetic changes accompanying the domestication of Pisum sativum: is there a common genetic basis to the 'domestication syndrome'for legumes? Ann. Bot. 100, 1017â1025 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1922">42</a></sup> and distinguishes the many types of beans that humans consume. We surveyed the Mesoamerican domestication candidates for genes previously shown to be associated with seed weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Van Daele, I. et al. A comparative study of seed yield parameters in Arabidopsis thaliana mutants and transgenics. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 488â500 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1926">43</a></sup> and used the whole-genome sequence for a genome-wide association study (GWAS; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>) to understand the genetic architecture of seed weight in modern Mesoamerican cultivars. We found 15 candidate genes previously shown to be involved in seed weight (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 19</a>). Among these are nearly all the components of the cytokinin synthesis and multiple-component phosphorelay regulatory system (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). Included are <i>Phvul.002G082400</i>, which encodes a protein that transmits the phosphosignal in response to regulators, and three type B response regulator transcription factors (<i>Phvul.003G017000</i>, <i>Phvul.003G110100</i> and <i>Phvul.009G088900</i>), which in turn activate a number of downstream genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hwang, I., Sheen, J. &amp; Muller, B. Cytokinin signaling networks. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 63, 353â380 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1952">44</a></sup>. An additional candidate gene, <i>Phvul.01G038800</i>, has orthologs that encode cytokinin oxidase/dehydrogenase proteins, which regulate the pathway by degrading active cytokinin. The relevance of these genes as candidate loci associated with seed weight is supported by work in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, where orthologs of the candidate genes in the cytokinin pathway have been shown in transgenic studies to regulate seed size and/or weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Van Daele, I. et al. A comparative study of seed yield parameters in Arabidopsis thaliana mutants and transgenics. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 488â500 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18809e1963">43</a></sup>. In contrast, however, none of these genes were Andean domestication candidates.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genome-wide association analysis of seed weight."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Genome-wide association analysis of seed weight.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="675" height="1251"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) A 280-member panel of Mesoamerican cultivars was grown in 4 locations in the United States. Phenotypic data were coupled with 34,799 SNP markers and analyzed using a mixed-model analysis that controlled for population structure and genotype relatedness. (<b>b</b>) A close-up view of the GWAS results for seed weight and linkage disequilibrium (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) around a 1.23-Mb Mesoamerican sweep window on Pv07. The positions of candidate genes for domestication are noted by asterisks above the GWAS display. The candidates range from <i>Phvul.007G094299</i> to <i>Phvul.007G.99700</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Note</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3008/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>GWAS analysis for seed weight confirmed three of these domestication candidates. It was not possible to confirm the other 12 candidates by GWAS because Mesoamerican domestication reduced diversity to near homozygosity, such that associations could not be found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 20</a>). GWAS analysis was able to place 75 domestication candidate genes within 50 kb of a SNP significantly (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 Ã 10<sup>â4</sup>) associated with seed weight, and a significantly associated SNP was found within eight candidate genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 21</a>). One sweep window on Pv07 (9.662â10.662 Mb) contained 33 domestication candidates and was located in a GWAS peak that exhibited extensive linkage disequilibrium (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). By GWAS, we also detected candidate genes for seed weight that resulted from modern breeding of the common bean. These included 15 improvement-related genes previously shown to be associated with seed weight, 5 of which function in the cytokinin regulation/degradation pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 22</a>). Finally, three genes in complete linkage disequilibrium with equally significant association (<i>P</i> = 6.3 Ã 10<sup>â6</sup>) were located in a Pv07 QTL for seed weight that has been replicated in many experiments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="GonzÃ¡lez, A.M., De la Fuente, M., De Ron, A.M. &amp; Santalla, M. Protein markers and seed size variation in common bean segregating populations. Mol. Breed. 25, 723â740 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2033">45</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Common bean is the most important grain legume for human consumption and is an especially nutrient-dense food in developing parts of the world. Improvement of common bean will require a more fundamental understanding of the genetic basis of how it responds to biotic and abiotic stresses. The clustering of resistance-associated genes in a few genomic locations suggests that stacking resistances between clusters should be relatively easy but that stacking multiple resistance genes located within a single physical cluster and then combining these traits by breeding may prove more challenging. The observation that the dual domestication events for common bean had few selective sweeps in common leads us to posit that domestication, previously thought to typically be associated with selection at a few major loci, can also be achieved via multiple genetic pathways resulting in similar or the same phenotypes (for example, seed size). In addition, the lack of correspondence between selective sweeps in domestication and genetic bottlenecks imposed by breeding indicates that domestication-derived traits were fixed early and that subsequent selection was likely on traits for local adaptation and desired seed and plant traits. Together, these findings provide information on regions of the genome that have undergone intense selection, either during domestication or early improvement, and thus provide targets for future crop improvement efforts, as valuable alleles will have been lost during early selection.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Sequencing.</h3><p>The majority of <i>de novo</i> genome sequencing reads were collected with standard sequencing protocols provided by the manufacturer on Roche 454 XLR and Illumina HiSeq 2000 machines at the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute in Walnut Creek, California. Two types of linear 454 data were collected, standard XLR data (31 runs; 10.7 Gb) and FLX+ data (8.5 runs; 5.615 Gb). Six different paired 454 libraries were created, three libraries with average insert sizes of 2.8â4.8 kb, 1 library with average insert size of 8.0 kb, 1 library with average insert size of 9.2 kb, 1 library with average insert size of 11.9 kb and 1 library with average insert size of 12.2 kb, and were sequenced by standard XLR (26.5 runs; 6.282 Gb of useable data). Two standard 400-bp fragment libraries were sequenced at 2 Ã 101 bp (four channels; 135.8 Gb) on an Illumina HiSeq 2000. Two fosmid libraries (328,704 reads; 223.9 Mb) with 35.0-kb and 36.0-kb insert sizes and 3 BAC libraries (89,017 reads; 55.1 Mb) with 127.0-kb, (92,160 reads; 65.9 Mb), 135.3-kb (81,408 reads; 57.6 Mb) and 122.0-kb average insert sizes were sequenced on both ends with Sanger sequencing for a total of 591,289 Sanger reads of 402.5 Mb of high-quality sequence. Fosmid-end and BAC-end sequence data were collected using standard protocols at the HudsonAlpha Institute in Huntsville, Alabama, and at the Arizona Genomics Institute in Tucson, Arizona. Sixty <i>P. vulgaris</i> genotypes representing 30 wild Mesoamerican and 30 wild Andean individuals were pooled into 2 sequencing libraries, and 54Ã and 4.9Ã genome equivalents were collected on a HiSeq 2000 with unamplified libraries. Similarly, 100 genotypes from 6 individual landrace classes, selected from a structure analysis, were pooled into 6 libraries, and sequencing depths from 3.4 to 7.1Ã were achieved.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Construction of the genetic map.</h3><p>We obtained 19,619 Mb of 121-bp paired-end Illumina Genome Analyzer IIx short reads from a diverse set of genotypes for common bean. Reads were aligned to the genome reference sequences for common bean with 14Ã coverage, and SNPs were called using CASAVA1.7 software (Illumina, 2010) with the default settings. After filtering out A/T or G/C SNPs, SNPs with Ns in the 60 nt of flanking sequence and SNPs residing within 25 nt of another SNP, a total of 992,682 SNPs remained. Using these SNPs, an Illumina Infinium BeadChip (BARCBEAN6K_1 with 5,232 SNPs) was designed. The SNPs for BARCBEAN6K_1 were selected to optimize polymorphism among the various common bean market classes, and, when possible, SNPs were targeted to sequence scaffolds (&gt;10 kb) in an early <i>P. vulgaris</i> assembly. A mapping population of 267 F<sub>2</sub> progeny from a cross of the common bean cultivars Stampede and Red Hawk developed at North Dakota State University was genotyped with the BARCBEAN6K_1 BeadChip. An additional BeadChip (BARCBEAN6K_2 with 5,514 SNPs) was designed using the same steps as with the <i>P. vulgaris</i> v0.9 assembly, with markers selected to anchor and orient additional scaffold sequences and used to type the same population. Both BeadChips and 261 SSR markers were also used to genotype 88 F<sub>5</sub>-derived RILs from the cross of the Stampede and Red Hawk cultivars. SSRs were selected from sequence scaffolds in the <i>P. vulgaris</i> 8Ã assembly, PCR markers were designed and fragment length polymorphisms were assessed as described in Song <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Song, Q. et al. Abundance of SSR motifs and development of candidate polymorphic SSR markers (BARCSOYSSR_1. 0) in soybean. Crop Sci. 50, 1950â1960 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2088">46</a></sup>. Linkage maps were constructed using JoinMap 4.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Van Ooijen, J. JoinMap 4. Software for the Calculation of Genetic Linkage Maps in Experimental Populations (Kyazma, Wageningen, The Netherlands, 2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2091">47</a>) software on the basis of the 6,531 polymorphic SNPs from these 2 BeadChips and 484 SNP loci that were genotyped with the Illumina GoldenGate assay at the US Department of AgricultureâAgricultural Research Service in Beltsville, Maryland<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Hyten, D.L. et al. High-throughput SNP discovery and assay development in common bean. BMC Genomics 11, 475 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2095">48</a></sup>, as well as 261 SSR markers and 25 framework markers. The final map contained 7,276 SSR and SNP markers arranged in 11 linkage groups via framework markers.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly and construction of pseudomolecule chromosomes.</h3><p>Before assembly, reads corresponding to organelle DNA were removed by screening against identified fragments of mitochondria, chloroplast and rDNA. For Roche 454 linear reads, any read &lt;200 bp in length was discarded. Roche 454 paired reads were split into pairs, and any pair with a read shorter than 50 bp was discarded. An additional deduplication step was applied to the 454 paired libraries that identified and retained only one copy of each PCR duplicate. All remaining 454 reads were compared against 24.1 Gb of trimmed HiSeq 2000 V3 reads from two separate libraries, and any insertion-deletions in the 454 reads were corrected to match the Illumina alignments. Before assembly, 454 reads that contained &gt;80% 24-mers that occurred â¥400 times in the data set were removed to reduce improper assembly of transposon sequences. Sequence reads were assembled using our modified version of Arachne v.20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Jaffe, D.B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91â96 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2106">49</a>) with parameters maxcliq1 = 250 and BINGE_AND_PURGE = True, bless = False BINGE_AND_PURGE = True lap_ratio = 0.8 max_bad_look = 2000 (note: Arachne error correction was on). An additional filtering step to remove contigs of &lt;300 bp in length or with fewer than four reads was applied. This produced 1,627 scaffold sequences, with a scaffold L50 value of 6.0 Mb; 171 scaffolds were greater than 100 kb in length, and the total genome size was 474.3 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Scaffolds were screened against bacterial proteins, organelle sequences and the GenBank nr database and were removed if found to be a contaminant. Additional scaffolds were removed if they (i) consisted of &gt;95% 24-mers that occurred four other times in scaffolds greater than 50 kb in length, (ii) contained only unanchored RNA sequences or (iii) were less than 1 kb in length.</p><p>The 7,015 markers from the genetic map were aligned to the assembly using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Kent, W.J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2116">50</a></sup> (parameters: -<i>t</i> = dna -q = dna -minScore = 200 âextendThroughN). Positions of SSR markers were determined using E-PCR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Schuler, G.D. Sequence mapping by electronic PCR. Genome Res. 7, 541â550 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2123">51</a></sup>. Scaffolds were broken if they contained linkage group or syntenic discontiguity coincident with an area of low BAC or fosmid coverage. A total of 71 breaks were executed and 284 joins were made to form the final assembly consisting of 11 pseudomolecule chromosomes. Each chromosome join was padded with 10,000 Ns to indicate unsized map joins. The final assembly contained 708 scaffolds (41,391 contigs) that cover 472.5 Mb of the genome with a contig N50 value of 39.5 kb and a scaffold N50 value of 50.4 Mb.</p><p>Completeness of the euchromatic portion of the genome assembly was assessed using 108,874 <i>P. vulgaris</i> EST sequences obtained from GenBank. These sequences were aligned to the assembly to estimate completeness using BLAT (parameters: -<i>t</i> = dna -q = rna âextendThroughN). Alignments that comprised â¥90% base-pair identity and â¥85% EST coverage were retained. The screened alignments indicated that 102,254 of the 108,874 cDNAs (93.92%) aligned to the assembly. At least 30% of the ESTs that did not align were bacterial or fungal contaminants. In addition, BAC clones from euchromatic regions and moderately to highly repetitive regions were sequenced and compared to the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Figs. 19â23</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Annotation.</h3><p>We constructed 43,627 transcript assemblies from about 727 million reads of paired-end Illumina RNA-seq data. These transcript assemblies were constructed using PERTRAN (S.S., unpublished data). We built 47,464 transcript assemblies using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies.
