Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2147">52</a></sup> from 79,630 <i>P. vulgaris</i> Sanger ESTs and the RNA-seq transcript assemblies. Loci were identified by transcript assembly alignments and/or EXONERATE alignments of peptides from <i>Arabidopsis</i>, poplar, <i>Medicago truncatula,</i> grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>) and rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) peptides to the repeat-soft-masked genome using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2167">53</a></sup> on the basis of a transposon database developed as part of this project (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Sec22">URLs</a>) with up to 2,000-bp extension on both ends, unless they extended into another locus on the same strand. Gene models were predicted by the homology-based predictors FGENESH+ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2173">53</a>), FGENESH_EST (similar to FGENESH+; EST as splice-site and intron input instead of peptide/translated ORF) and GenomeScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yeh, R.-F., Lim, L.P. &amp; Burge, C.B. Computational inference of homologous gene structures in the human genome. Genome Res. 11, 803â816 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2177">54</a></sup>. The highest scoring predictions for each locus were selected using multiple positive factors, including EST and peptide support, and one negative factorâoverlap with repeats. Selected gene predictions were improved by PASA, including by adding UTRs, correcting splicing and adding alternative transcripts. PASA-improved gene model peptides were subjected to peptide homology analysis with the above-mentioned proteomes to obtain Cscore values and peptide coverage. Cscore is the ratio of the peptide BLASTP score to the mutual best hit BLASTP score, and peptide coverage is the highest percentage of peptide aligned to the best homolog. A transcript was selected if its Cscore value was greater than or equal to 0.5 and its peptide coverage was greater than or equal to 0.5 or if it had EST coverage but the proportion of its coding sequence overlapping repeats was less than 20%. For gene models where greater than 20% of the coding sequence overlapped with repeats, the Cscore value was required to be at least 0.9 and homology coverage was required to be at least 70% to be selected. Selected gene models were subjected to Pfam analysis, and gene models whose encoded peptide contained more than 30% Pfam transposon element domains were removed. The final gene set consisted of 27,197 protein-coding genes and 31,638 protein-coding transcripts.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Repeat analysis.</h3><p>In addition to the genome sequence, 15 publicly available BAC sequences for common bean were also downloaded from GenBank for a total of 2.2 Mb of sequence, including from accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=DQ205649">DQ205649</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=DQ323045">DQ323045</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=FJ817289">FJ817289</a>â<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=FJ817291">FJ817291</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GU215957">GU215957</a>â<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=GU215966">GU215966</a>. Transposon annotation was conducted using different methods according to the sequence structures and transposases of various transposons. To annotate LTR retrotransposons, the genome sequence was screened with LTR_Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Fornara, F., de Montaigu, A. &amp; Coupland, G. SnapShot: control of flowering in Arabidopsis thaliana. Cell 141, 550 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2233">35</a></sup> using default parameters, except that we set a 50-bp minimum LTR length and 50-bp minimum distance between LTRs. All predicted LTR retrotransposons were manually inspected to eliminate incorrectly predicted sequences, including tandem repeats, nested transposons, incomplete DNA transposons and other sequences. The internal sequences of LTR retrotransposons were used to perform BLASTX and/or BLASTP searches to define superfamilies: <i>Ty</i>1-<i>copia</i>, <i>Ty</i>3-<i>gypsy</i> or other. LINEs (long interspersed elements) were predicted on the basis of the non-LTR retrotransposase and polyA sequences. SINEs (short interspersed elements) were annotated with the polyA structure feature and combined with BLAST searches. To find DNA transposons, conserved domains for transposases from different reported superfamilies were used as queries to search the common bean genome. The matching sequences and flanking sequence (10 kb on each side) were extracted to conduct BLASTN searches to identify complete DNA transposons by terminal inverted repeats (TIRs) and target size duplication (TSD). Furthermore, MITEs-Hunter software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chen, H. et al. Arabidopsis CULLIN4-damaged DNA binding protein 1 interacts with CONSTITUTIVELY PHOTOMORPHOGENIC1âSUPPRESSOR OF PHYA complexes to regulate photomorphogenesis and flowering time. Plant Cell 22, 108â123 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2250">36</a></sup> was also used to identify DNA elements. The annotated transposons and two reported LTR retrotransposons, pva1-118d24-re-5 (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=FJ402927">FJ402927</a>) and Tpv2-6 (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AJ005762">AJ005762</a>), were combined and used as a transposon library to screen the genome using RepeatMasker with default settings except that we used the 'nolow' option to avoid masking low-complexity DNA or simple repeats. Transposons were summarized according to names, subclasses and classes, and overlapping regions in the RepeatMasker output file were counted once (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>To estimate the insertion times of LTR retrotransposons, the 5â² and 3â² LTRs for each full-length LTR retroelement were aligned and used to calculate the nucleotide divergence rate with the Kimura-2 parameter using MEGA 4. The insertion date (<i>T</i>) was estimated with the formula <i>T</i> = <i>K</i>/2<i>r</i>, where <i>K</i> is the average number of substitutions per aligned site and <i>r</i> is an average substitution rate. We used the average substitution rate of 1.3 Ã 10<sup>â8</sup> substitutions per synonymous site per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404â12410 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2295">55</a></sup> to calibrate the insertion times.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Identification of disease resistance genes.</h3><p>NL proteins were identified in an iterative process. First, an HMM (Hidden Markov model) search of the predicted protein sequences identified sequences containing the NB-ARC domain. The 'trusted cutoff' of the NB-ARC domain HMM (PF00931) established by Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Finn, R.D. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D211âD222 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2307">56</a></sup> was used as the threshold for detecting NBS domains. We identified 398 predicted proteins corresponding to 342 annotated genes that encoded homologs of NL proteins. To identify diverse homologs, all the NL predicted protein sequences were used as queries for TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Altschul, S.F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2311">57</a></sup> against the entire genome. All resulting sequences (<i>E</i> value &lt; 1 Ã 10<sup>â10</sup>) were manually inspected using Artemis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Rutherford, K. et al. Artemis: sequence visualization and annotation. Bioinformatics 16, 944â945 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2320">58</a></sup>. This procedure identified an additional 38 putative NL genes that were not part of the genome annotation. A new identifier was created for each missing gene (with last digits set as 50). NL genes were assessed manually in Artemis software for the presence of sequences encoding TIR (PF01582), NB-ARC (PF00931) and LRR (PF00560, PF07723, PF07725, PF12799, PF13306, PF13516, PF13504 and PF13855) domains with HMMer using the trusted cutoffs defined in Pfam. Coiled-coil domains were identified using Coils<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lupas, A., Van Dyke, M. &amp; Stock, J. Predicting coiled coils from protein sequences. Science 252, 1162â1164 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2325">59</a></sup> with a 14-amino-acid search window and a cutoff score of 2.9. Artemis was used for further manual analysis. Gene models with stop codons and/or frameshifts were classified as pseudogenes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Development of wild and landrace pools for sequencing of common bean.</h3><p>Initially, 126 wild and 179 landrace genotypes, collected from the full geographic range of the species, were scored with 22 indel markers distributed throughout the genome. A Bayesian analysis was performed on the genotype data within each of the two groups using STRUCTURE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Falush, D., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J.K. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data: linked loci and correlated allele frequencies. Genetics 164, 1567â1587 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2337">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Pritchard, J.K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945â959 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2340">61</a></sup> with the parameters outlined previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="McClean, P.E. et al. Population structure and genetic differentiation among the USDA common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) core collection. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 59, 499â515 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2344">62</a></sup>. For the wild genotypes where <i>k</i> is the number of populations, <i>k</i> = 2 best fit the data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software STRUCTURE: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611â2620 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2354">63</a></sup>, and, for the landraces, <i>k</i> = 6 defined 3 Mexican subpopulations, 1 Central American subpopulations and 2 Andean subpopulations. A genotype was assigned to a subpopulation if its subpopulation parentage was &gt;70%. DNA pools for resequencing were created by selecting individuals with high subpopulation membership (&gt;98% for wild subpopulations and &gt;90% for landrace subpopulations; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). In adopting other approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Rubin, C.-J. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals loci under selection during chicken domestication. Nature 464, 587â591 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2365">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Doebley, J.F., Gaut, B.S. &amp; Smith, B.D. The molecular genetics of crop domestication. Cell 127, 1309â1321 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2368">31</a></sup>, several individual-pool SNP data were combined with other pool SNP data to create a pool SNP data set representing a putative ancestral state.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Pooled DNA sequencing and SNP identification.</h3><p>DNA from each of these pools was sequenced to <span class="stix">â¼</span>4Ã depth using Illumina technology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Each read was mapped to the v1.0 version of the assembled reference genome using Burrows-Wheeler Aligner (BWA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2383">64</a></sup> with the maximum edit distance set to 8. All reads with a mapping quality score of less than 25 were discarded. An mpileup file was created for each sequenced pool using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2387">65</a></sup> with the âBA options. VarScan 2.2.10 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Koboldt, D.C. et al. VarScan 2: somatic mutation and copy number alteration discovery in cancer by exome sequencing. Genome Res. 22, 568â576 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2390">66</a>) used the mpileup file for SNP calling with the following parameters: minimum coverage = 5, minimum consensus quality = 25 and minimum variant frequency = 0.01. To further reduce SNP call quality, SNPs were discarded (i) if the reference or variant allele was an N; (ii) if more than one variant allele was observed; and (iii) if the variant allele was a single-nucleotide indel. The minimum number of reads required for the reference or variant allele was three. The number of SNPs ranged from 8,890,318 for the wild Mesoamerican pool to 1,397,405 for the Peru landrace pool (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Among wild genotypes, 10,158,326 SNPs were observed, whereas the Mesoamerican landrace genotypes contained 9,661,807 SNPs and the Andean landrace genotypes contained 3,154,648 SNPs. For individual and combined pools, the proportion of SNPs found within genes was <span class="stix">â¼</span>16%, indicating that genes were not disproportionately prone to more (or less) variation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Demographic modeling.</h3><p>To minimize bias in demographic inferences due to selection, we used neutral sites defined to be at least 5 kb away from a gene (as annotated in the gff3 file v1.0) and not located in repetitive regions. The number of different haplotypes for each pooled sample was close to 30. Data were thus down-sampled to 25 haplotypes for each pool via hypergeometric projection (random sampling of 25 alleles without replacement), from which the joint allele frequency spectrum (jAFS) was derived. To eliminate spurious singletons, we excluded sites appearing as singletons in either of the two pools, resulting in a total of 663,000 polymorphic sites for jAFS.</p><p>We compared different demographic models on the basis of the relative log likelihoods of the models given the observed site frequency spectrum. Asymmetric migration rates were assumed in the model (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). To infer model parameters, we ran Î´aÎ´i simulations with different starting points in an eight-dimensional parameter space until convergence was achieved. Parameter values for the best-fit model are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 13</a>, using a base substitution rate <i>Î¼</i> = 8.46 Ã 10<sup>â9</sup> substitutions/bp/year (S.B.C., unpublished data) derived from silent sites. To estimate parameter uncertainties, we divided the genome into 10-cM segments and performed 100 bootstraps on the chromosome segments. Confidence intervals were derived on the basis of simulation results for the bootstrapped samples (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 13</a>) as were comparisons between model prediction and observed data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Figs. 24</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">25</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Population genetics statistics.</h3><p>Several population genetics statistics were calculated in 100-kb/10-kb and 10-kb/2-kb sliding windows and each gene in each DNA pool. Any window or gene with &gt;50% Ns was excluded, and all statistics were based on the number of non-N nucleotides in the window. Nucleotide diversity (<i>Ï</i>, the average number of nucleotide differences per site between two DNA sequences chosen randomly from the sample population; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Tajima, F. Evolutionary relationship of DNA sequences in finite populations. Genetics 105, 437â460 (1983)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2438">67</a>) was calculated using the following formula:</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw500/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Equ1_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt=""></div></div><p>Here <i>x</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> and <i>x</i><sub><i>j</i></sub> are the respective frequencies of the <i>i</i>th and <i>j</i>th sequences, <i>Ï</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> is the number of nucleotide differences per nucleotide site between the <i>i</i>th and <i>j</i>th sequences, and <i>n</i> is the number of sequences in the sample. The Watterson estimate (<i>Î¸</i><sub>w</sub>; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Watterson, G.A. On the number of segregating sites in genetical models without recombination. Theor. Popul. Biol. 7, 256â276 (1975)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2490">68</a>), which is an estimation of population mutation rate, was calculated on the basis of the number of segregating sites using the formula</p><div id="Equ2" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw500/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Equ2_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt=""></div></div><p>where <i>S</i> is the number of segregating sites and</p><div id="Equ3" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw500/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Equ3_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt=""></div></div><p>Tajima's <i>D</i>, calculated as described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tajima, F. Statistical method for testing the neutral mutation hypothesis by DNA polymorphism. Genetics 123, 585â595 (1989)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2519">69</a>. <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Hudson, R.R., Slatkin, M. &amp; Maddison, W. Estimation of levels of gene flow from DNA sequence data. Genetics 132, 583â589 (1992)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2526">70</a>) is a measure of population differentiation estimated from the average pairwise differences between chromosomes in each analysis panel compared to the combined samples as described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="International HapMap Consortium. A haplotype map of the human genome. Nature 437, 1299â1320 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2529">71</a></p><div id="Equ4" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw500/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3008/MediaObjects/41588_2014_BFng3008_Equ4_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt=""></div></div><p>where <i>x</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> is the estimated frequency of the minor allele at SNP <i>i</i> in population <i>j</i>, <i>n</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> is the number of genotyped chromosomes at that position and <i>n</i><sub><i>j</i></sub> is the number of chromosomes analyzed in that population. The lack of the <i>j</i> subscript in the denominator indicates that statistics <i>n</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> and <i>x</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> are calculated across the combined data sets.</p><p>The relative diversity among two pooled samples was compared by a nucleotide diversity ratio (<i>Ï</i>) between the two pools for each window or gene. For example, the ratio <i>Ï</i><sub>MA-wild</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>MA-landrace</sub> measures the relative difference in diversity between the Mesoamerican wild gene pool and the Mesoamerican landrace gene pool. Similarly, an <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> value was calculated for each window and gene to compare the differentiation between any two pools.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identifying selected windows and genes and defining sweep windows.</h3><p>A composite scoring system was used to determine whether a 10-kb/2-kb sliding or gene window was under selection. This approach is similar to the one applied for silk moth where a reduction in nucleotide diversity and Tajima's <i>D</i> was applied to discover domestication-related genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Xia, Q. et al. Complete resequencing of 40 genomes reveals domestication events and genes in silkworm (Bombyx). Science 326, 433â436 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2614">72</a></sup>. Here a 10-kb/2-kb window or a gene was considered a selection window or domestication candidate gene if it was in the upper 90% of the pool's empirical distribution for the <i>Ï</i><sub>wild</sub>/<i>Ï</i><sub>landrace</sub> ratio and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> statistics. The cutoff values for various comparisons can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3008#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 18</a>. All 10-kb/2-kb selection windows within 40 kb of each other were merged in a 'sweep window'. The numbers of domestication candidates and total genes were calculated for each sweep window.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Annotating candidates for seed weight and size in common bean.</h3><p>We used the <i>Arabidopsis</i> protein sequence for all genes found to be associated with seed weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Van Daele, I. et al. A comparative study of seed yield parameters in Arabidopsis thaliana mutants and transgenics. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 488â500 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2645">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Kesavan, M., Song, J.T. &amp; Seo, H.S. Seed size: a priority trait in cereal crops. Physiol. Plant. 147, 113â120 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2648">73</a></sup> as queries for a BLASTP analysis of a database of the common bean proteins. We identified 141 common bean gene models with 50% identity and 80% coverage that matched 70% of the query length, and these inherited the <i>Arabidopsis</i> names for the gene associated with seed weight.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Association mapping.</h3><p>In total, 271 diverse modern common bean varieties from the Mesoamerican gene pool were grown in replicated field trials by North Dakota State University, Michigan State University, the University of Nebraska and Colorado State University bean breeding programs. Each variety was genotyped with 34,799 SNPs. Missing data were imputed in fastPHASE 1.3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Scheet, P. &amp; Stephens, M. A fast and flexible statistical model for large-scale population genotype data: applications to inferring missing genotypes and haplotypic phase. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 78, 629â644 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2662">74</a>) using likelihood-based imputation. Adjusted means for seed weight data across all locations were calculated using the MIXED procedure in SAS9.3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="SAS Institute, Inc. SAS 9.3 Language Reference: Concepts, Second Edition (SAS Institute, Inc., Cary, NC, 2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2665">75</a>), where the genotype was the fixed effect and all other factors were considered to be random.</p><p>A mixed linear model (MLM) controlling for population relatedness was used to conduct the GWAS. The mixed model used was from Yu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Yu, J. et al. A unified mixed-model method for association mapping that accounts for multiple levels of relatedness. Nat. Genet. 38, 203â208 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2675">76</a></sup>, and the equation used was <i>y</i> = <i>xÎ²</i> + <i>zÎ¼</i> + <i>É</i>, where <i>y</i> is the seed weight phenotype, <i>xÎ²</i> indicates the genotype fixed effect, <i>zÎ¼</i> represents the kinship coefficient as the random effect and <i>É</i> is a vector of residual effects. An identity-by-state (IBS) kinship matrix (EMMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Kang, H.M. et al. Efficient control of population structure in model organism association mapping. Genetics 178, 1709â1723 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2704">77</a></sup>) was used to control for population relatedness. The kinship matrix was calculated using marker loci with pairwise <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> &gt; 0.5. The linkage disequilibrium (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) between all marker loci was calculated in PLINK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559â575 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2717">78</a></sup> using a minor allele frequency of 0.1. The EMMA kinship matrix and the GWAS were calculated in the genome association and prediction integrated tool (GAPIT) package in R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Lipka, A.E. et al. GAPIT: genome association and prediction integrated tool. Bioinformatics 28, 2397â2399 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2721">79</a></sup>, without P3D and compression. Only markers with minor allele frequency of 0.1 or greater were considered in the GWAS results. Protein sequences for <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes associated with seed weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Van Daele, I. et al. A comparative study of seed yield parameters in Arabidopsis thaliana mutants and transgenics. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 488â500 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2729">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Kesavan, M., Song, J.T. &amp; Seo, H.S. Seed size: a priority trait in cereal crops. Physiol. Plant. 147, 113â120 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3008#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d18809e2732">73</a></sup> were used as queries for a BLASTP analysis against a database of common bean proteins. We identified 141 common bean gene models with 50% identity and 80% coverage that matched 70% of the query length, and these inherited the <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene names.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">URLs.</h3><p>Food and Agricultural Organization of the United Nations (FAO) statistics, <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/site/291/default.aspx">http://faostat.fao.org/site/291/default.aspx</a>; Plant DNA C-values Database, <a href="http://www.kew.org/cvalues/">http://www.kew.org/cvalues/</a>; Phytozome transposon database, <a href="http://www.Phytozome.net/">http://www.Phytozome.net/</a>; RepeatMasker, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>; MEGA 4, <a href="http://www.megasoftware.net/mega4/">http://www.megasoftware.net/mega4/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Accession codes.</h3><p>Assembly and annotation are available at <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/commonbean.php">http://www.phytozome.net/commonbean.php</a> and have been deposited in GenBank under accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=ANNZ01000000">ANNZ01000000</a>.</p></div></div></section>
